B007EC6FIS|5.0|I couldn't be more stunned by the three reviews before mine. \\"Strangeland\\" is an exceptionally strong return to solid ground for Keane, whose first two albums set the bar. I do agree that the third album, \\"Perfect Symmetry,\\" as well as the fourth, \\"Night Train,\\" were disappointments with only a few standout tracks each. That's partly why I love \\"Strangeland\\" so much -- it's a return to the sublime Keane sound of old, yet burnished with maturity and confidence.  Even if this were the first Keane album I'd ever heard, I'd be feeling just as good about it. High points, in my opinion, are \\"You Are Young,\\" \\"In Your Own Time\\" and \\"Strangeland,\\" but it's hard for me to single those out when nearly all 16 tracks are so good. There is NOTHING I want to skip here, and that's rare for me. \\"On the Road\\" and \\"Day Will Come\\" round out the top 5. As I listen to the album more, I suspect that \\"Black Rain,\\" \\"The Starting Line\\" and others will grow on me. The astonishly good b-side, \\"Myth,\\" is not included on the album, but I was able to get that elsewhere and thus complete the package.  Weakest tracks -- and these are still fine, just not memorable -- are \\"Neon River,\\" \\"Boys,\\" and \\"Sea Fog\\" -- I don't know why some listeners like \\"Sea Fog\\" so much, it's got a pretty sound but it's so boring compared to the rest of the tracks. If there's one complaint I'd make, it's that the songs are short; almost all of them are under 4 minutes. That's a shame, because with tunes this good, you really want to get into them and make them last. Fortunately, Keane gives us 16 songs here, so the album's total length is a very fine one hour. Bottom line: I'm really impressed by \\"Strangeland\\" and it will be stuck in my player for quite a while going forward.

0307951529|5.0|As someone who started a stationery and greeting card business on less than $100, I approached the $100 Startup with much interest. I was pleased to discover that there were many others out there like me, who had launched their businesses on little capital, and that there was a term for our types of businesses: \\"Microbusiness.\\"<br /><br />Guillebeau starts out with the assumption that you want a change in your life and want to work for yourself, but you don't know exactly what you want to do. He then guides you through the process of figuring out what it is you could do (the convergence between your skill set/passion and what others care about). The book then discusses how to market your business (by translating what your business provides into a benefit for the customer), how to discover what it is your customers really want and how to sell it to them, how to work from anywhere, how to price your product/service, how to launch the product/service, and how to tackle business growth. Along the way, Guillebeau provides concrete examples of people who have been there and done that, so to speak, and walks us through how these people have approached the above steps.<br /><br />What I found most valuable about the book was Guillebeau's insight into pricing. In my industry, we commonly hear that your wholesale price should be cost of materials + labor, multiplied by 2 or 2.2. However, Guillebeau recommends basing your pricing on benefits, not costs.<br /><br />I also found the guides to the one-page business plan and the 140 word mission statement very useful because it gives you a structure that doesn't appear so intimidating. I've definitely put off writing a business plan for weeks and months on end, and I imagine others are the same. Guillebeau also includes a \\"decision-making matrix\\" that allows you to evaluate the passion level, profitability, and effort of your ideas for the business, and ultimately, whether you should go through with them.<br /><br />If there is one negative feature of the book, I would say that all the success stories profiled in the book can be a little overwhelming (sometimes overwhelming the message of the chapter). They could also lead many people to think that starting a business is easy and with minimal time and effort, you could be raking in hundreds of thousands of dollars. Maybe in some cases that's true, but not in the majority of them.<br /><br />Guillebeau acknowledges that entrepreneurship is not for everyone, and sometimes it's best to pursue your passion on the side while earning a steady income. He also concludes by urging microbusiness owners to give back to their community and help others, and even gives names of specific charities to which he contributes.<br /><br />This is a great, inspiring guide to starting a business. I'd recommend reading it along with books that are specific to your industry.

Go see them if you get the chance.<br /><br />On that note, I thought I'd review the now legendary 'Greatest Hits' album. This album is probably the album that every casual Tom Petty fan owns. I'll admit it -- this was the album that got me hooked on Tom Petty. It had all of the songs I had heard on the radio up to that point and a handful of tracks that seldom got played on the radio but were just as good as the radio hits.<br /><br />This album contains just about everything the easily-pleased Petty fan would want. 'American Girl,' 'Breakdown,' 'I Need to Know,' 'Breakdown,' 'The Waiting,' 'Refugee,' 'You Got Lucky.' They're all here. If you just want the Petty songs that you hear on classic rock radio on a frequent basis and those songs only, look no further than 'Greatest Hits.' It collects them all for you in a nice little affordable package and in great sound. The sound quality here is superb, better than 90% of CDs that are released in this over-compressed MP3 world (the \\"loudness war,\\" for the layman).<br /><br />Now, I've laid out the positives, here are the negatives.<br /><br />I love 'Full Moon Fever.' It revived Petty's career in 1989. The fact is, though, that it is not a Tom Petty & The Heartbreakers album -- it is a Tom Petty album. Sure, Mike Campbell plays on every track. Yes, I get that. But the rest of the Heartbreakers are absent (for the most part). So does it really make sense to put these tracks on a \\"Tom Petty & the HEARTBREAKERS\\" album? There is nothing from 1987's 'Let Me Up (I've Had Enough),' which admittedly is not his best work, but it is still the Heartbreakers nonetheless. 'Jammin' Me' and 'Runaway Trains' were both minor hits, and both could fit comfortably on this disc.<br /><br />It would be the same thing as releasing a Creedence Clearwater Revival greatest hits album and adding John Fogerty's 'Centerfield' on it, or releasing a Cream greatest hits album and putting Eric Clapton's 'It's in the Way That You Use it' on it. It just doesn't make sense. Solo careers are very different than the actual band. So, MCA should have added more from the 1976 self-titled debut, a few tracks from 'Let Me Up (I've Had Enough)' and perhaps a fan favorite or two (personally, I'd love to see 'Two Gunslingers' show up on a Heartbreakers compilation one day) to make this truly The Heartbreakers' greatest hits.<br /><br />The better title for this disc would be 'Tom Petty's Greatest Hits.' If you were to call it that, you'd have to add something like 'End of the Line' or 'Handle with Care' from the Wilburys album (Warner owns the masters of that, but they'd probably loan them for this greatest-hits project). So really, if you are a die-hard fan, this is a frustrating disc. It's really only worth it for the two new songs -- 'Mary Jane's Last Dance' and their cover of Thunderclap Newman's 'Something in the Air,' both of which are phenomenal. Rick Rubin produced both tracks and did so masterfully.<br /><br />Overall, if you are a casual Tom Petty/Tom Petty & The Heartbreakers fan who just wants the hits and nothing but, than 'Greatest Hits' will suit you fine. Otherwise, this is a somewhat frustrating and baffling compilation that focuses more on Tom Petty than Tom Petty & The Heartbreakers. The bonus track added here is 1976's 'Anything That's Rock & Roll,' a great deep album cut from the band's first album. Not a bad choice, but don't spend a ton of money for just that one track (just some friendly advice). It's not like it's an earth-shattering, collector's only rare song as the bonus track. It's the same version that's available on the debut which almost every serious Petty & the Heartbreakers fan owns anyway.<br /><br />The new-for-1993 songs are worth the price of admission for this disc alone. But really, it does frustrate me that 'Let Me Up (I've Had Enough)' got completely skipped over while 'Full Moon Fever' got plenty of recognition. A truly baffling compilation.ous Petty & the Heartbreakers fan owns anyway.<br /><br />The new-for-1993 songs are worth the price of admission for this disc alone. But really, it does frustrate me that 'Let Me Up (I've Had Enough)' got completely skipped over while 'Full Moon Fever' got plenty of recognition. A truly baffling compilation.

0812968255|4.0|I have been reading and re-reading Marcus Aurelius' Meditations for years. The translation in the Penguin edition always suffered from a kind of stiff literalism. It was as if the translator wanted to make the Meditations a more formal work than it is.<br /><br />Gregory Hays, in his translation of the work, pitches the idea that the Meditations was never meant for publishing, and as such, varies in formal style and quality. He tries to capture this in English, by switching from somewhat stiff, philosophical language in some parts, to a more conversational tone in others. He wants us to see the Meditations for what he believes it is: a kind of self-help book the Marcus Aurelius' wrote for himself; a reminder of what his goals were and how to achieve them.<br /><br />This makes Hays' Meditations a much easier read than its predecessor; he gives Marcus' voice a freshness and immediacy that is missing in other translation. Most importantly, he give new life to the voice of a Roman emperor who lived nearly two-thousand years ago.

B000ICM0YY|5.0|Other reviewers have already pointed out that this is not a complete Beethoven sonata cycle but it certainly comes very close, with only five of the thirty two sonatas missing and, of them, only one \\"major\\" sonata (Op. 111) is lacking. All the \\"name\\" sonatas (plus many non-name ones) are here and for many people this will be their main concern. To most intents this is pretty-well complete. AND it offers a couple of bonuses in the \\"Eroica\\" Variations and the two early \\"Electoral Sonatas.<br /><br />The (near) cycle comes in a neat, compact package but with very little, unfortunately, in the way of notes or documentation. There is a brief essay on Gilels but nothing about the sonatas as such. Sound-quality is variable but generally good, a notch below Barenboim's DG cycle but leagues ahead of Kempff's 60's set. I can't agree, however, that it's anything like \\"amazing\\", as one reviewer has put it.<br /><br />This is probably the most technically authoritative recording ever made of these works. There seems to be nothing that Gilels can't simply toss off with aplomb. Not that there is any hint of overt virtuosity at play; in fact there's a sense of austere disdain about any need for \\"display\\". Gilels is a musician first and a pianist a very distant second; the piano is simply his vehicle for giving utterance to this music.<br /><br />This technical control means that there is never any ambiguity in Gilels' conception of these sonatas. His intent and his execution are one. The musical line and shape is always utterly clear and these recordings are unique in my experience for their elucidation of what the left hand is doing. With most pianists the bulk of the musical interest is concentrated in the right hand with the left relegated to some sort of fuzzy background; with Gilels you are always conscious that the left hand is very nearly an equal partner. I was continually surprised at how much \\"point\\" the left-hand \\"accompaniment\\" has in these recordings. It's never just an amorphous murk lurking behind the main melodic line.<br /><br />My only reservation is that there is a constant emotional detachment behind Gilels' playing. At times, indeed, his approach reminds me of a forensic surgeon doing an autopsy on a dead body. He sets out the musical shape in an overly objective and  distant way as though he detests the idea of any emotional display; there is little sense of ecstacy or reverence except insofar as the music compels it. Gilels never \\"milks\\" the music. He never attempts to make it \\"beautiful\\" or \\"dramatic\\" or \\"titanic\\", as many other interpreters try to play it. It's as though he thinks it far too fine and pure to bring merely human emotion into it and some listeners may find this inhibits their own response. They may admire rather than love it. Even so, I think this is an essential set for anyone who has fallen for these sonatas; it offers something that nobody else does to anything like the same extent.n amorphous murk lurking behind the main melodic line.<br /><br />My only reservation is that there is a constant emotional detachment behind Gilels' playing. Even so, I think this is an essential set for anyone who has fallen for these sonatas; it offers something that nobody else does to anything like the same extent.

Nice idea, right?: all three singles in replica picture sleeves in a nice box with a poster and 45 adaptor. I fell in love with it too, but some things bother me...<br />1) The price, naturally. For this kind of money, and considering that records are made in much smaler quantitiies, there's no reason that more care couldn't be taken in making these things.<br />3) Some of the mixes aren't the original single mixes. B006WAIUEK|5.0|As a long time Welsh fan, I suppose I could be considered a little biased - but really, in all honesty, this is one of the cult Scottish author's finest efforts to date.<br /><br />Skagboys revisits the sunny port of Leith and adjoining Edinburgh, and the eclectic cast of characters that made up Welsh's debut effort - the drugged-out classic Trainspotting. All your skeevy old pals are here... ever-acerbic Mark Renton, sweet natured Spud, scheming Sickboy and, naturally, the delightfully psychotic pugilist, Begbie. Only this time, we meet Leith's finest schemies in their early twenties - just as they're developing that nasty little heroin habit which was the focal point of Trainspotting.<br /><br />So yes, it's a prequel to Trainspotting, featuring characters that should be familiar to fans of the earlier book or its cinematic adaptation. But, as always, Welsh is not afraid to delve a little deeper. While on the surface a simple story of a hopeless descent into addiction, the novel also chronicles the sad devolution of the working class in dystopian mid-eighties Scotland.<br /><br />It's quite interesting, but also quite depressing, to bear witness to the slow ravaging of blue collar Scottish society, whose denizens turn to drugs and violence to numb the economic and social hardships incurred by the rise of Maggie's Farm. The novel also deals with the rampant incursion of AIDS into Edinburgh - once Europe's AIDS capital - which spreads all too rapidly through shared needle use and illicit sex.<br /><br />If you're a fan of Trainspotting, or it's excellent sequel Porno (which revisits our anti-heroes in their mid thirties) then I simply can't recommend this read enough. In the off chance you've read some of Welsh's other works, and haven't got around to Trainspotting, then I'd advise you to start here. Be warned, though - as with all the author's works, this is definitely NOT for the faint of heart.<br /><br />Vintage Welsh. It's a good thing.

At several points, I was dabbing away actual tears.<br /><br />War Horse, in simple terms, is the story of a thoroughbred named Joey who finds himself serving as a save-the-family-and-its-farm plough horse, then impressed into the  British Army on the eve of World War One as a cavalry officer's mount, his capture by the Germans, rescue of two conscript brothers, and his own rescue in turn by a young French girl and her grandfather for a sort of idyllic breather before heading back to war. The movie's climactic scene is set in a dark and deadly no-man's land between the British and German trenches where Joey triggers an act of compassion by two soldiers who, although enemies, find a common humanity through a horse.  Joey's journey is a string of such encounters, with his life touching humans from farm to war and back full circle.<br /><br />The Blu-ray set of War Horse includes a fascinating assortment of making-of productions, most of them on a separate disc. We get a close look at Spielberg's noted attention to detail, lavished on the film by a set of colleagues he has assembled over the years, among them music-master John Williams and producer Kathleen Kennedy. It was Kennedy who broached the idea of War Horse as a movie to Spielberg after seeing the London production of the play. We see how an officer of the Humane Society helps ensure the safety of the animals who variously play Joey and the rest of the war horses  and include a comedic farm goose. And we see how the inspiring vistas of the British countryside of Dartmoor also double for the fields and villages of France.<br /><br />We also learn how the sound designer created the stunning soundtrack.  Joey's vocalizations include the slowed-down sounds of miniature horses and the teeth-clicking chatter of affection the designer noticed his wife's horse makes whenever she approaches. In one pivotal scene, Joey's confrontation with a tank, a clattering new horse of war, there is no music, only the sounds of the horse and machine.<br /><br />The photography is appropriately spectacular throughout. One panoramic sunset over the Dartmoor countryside brings to mind the vivid burning of Atlanta from Gone With the Wind.<br /><br />In the whole two-hour-plus production, I did find one minor annoyance: the fake battlefield snow flakes toward the end. I kept waiting for somebody to choke on a mouthful of whatever the puffy plastic-looking flakes were. Instead of falling naturally toward the ground, they swirled sideways and upward. Why can't somebody come up with something that if not real snow, at least looks it. (See White Christmas, 1954)<br /><br />Much more real: the rest of War Horse, particularly its compassion.

I wanted to love this author. I wanted to walk beside this woman on her journey of self-discovery and live the emotions she lived, and feel the transformation she was bound to undergo. I wanted to feel her boots on my feet, taste her fears in my mouth, and nod in understanding at each new revelation that came her way. What I found is that I couldn't keep focussed on the story for more than a paragraph at a time. If any character transformation occurred, it must have occurred in the last few pages of the book, a long way past where I stopped reading. I trudged and I trudged and I trudged right along beside the author as far as I could go, but eventually I was compelled to toss my metaphorical boots over the edge of a cliff and bring my journey through the pages ofWild to a premature end.

Your skill sets, NOW, are what you can use to make a business. (It's not about obtaining another degree, or more skills, or more money -- you can get started).<br /><br />* It's not enough to have passion. Your passions have to intersect with the creation of a product that is also useful and valuable to someone else. The good news is that you can find that.<br /><br />* Even location independent workers still work. The myth of the permanent vacation is construed. You have to do the work.<br /><br />* Having one job in an uncertain economy may be a much riskier endeavor than having multiple streams of income or running your own business. In today's economy, diversifying your work may be the key to success.<br /><br />* At the same time, you don't have to quit your day job. It's not about the drama and the glitz of walking away in a huff; it's about making what you have work, and hustling on the side if you have to. 0307949486|4.0|I realize that I'm jumping on the \\"Dragon Tattoo\\" bandwagon rather late. To be honest, I resisted reading this book for a long time, because I'm a snob and I tend to distrust popular trends. But I finally picked up \\"Tattoo\\" at an airport bookstore (where it was by far the least offensive thing on display), and I'm glad that I did, because this is a pretty awesome book. Not a classic or anything, but a fun read.<br /><br />What really surprised me about Larsson's book is that it's light on action, and heavy on exposition and atmosphere -- which is fine by me. The central figure in the plot, Mikael Blomkvist (the girl with the tattoo is really the secondary protagonist), is an investigative journalist whose hobbies include exposing shady corporate figures and sleeping around a lot. Most of the book consists of Blomkvist investigating the decades-old disapperance of a woman from a wealthy family, so page after page is dedicated to him interviewing various witnesses/suspects and combing through newspaper archives. If this all sounds boring to you, you'll probably find the book itself boring; but I generally enjoyed following Blomkvist's investigation, and I thought the character himself had a charming low-key appeal.<br /><br />Another surprising aspect of the book is its political content. Larsson wears his (left-wing, by U.S. standards) politics on his sleeve, and uses the book as a soapbox for condemning corporate corruption and calling attention to various forms of violence and abuse against women. (The book's original Swedish title, \\"Men Who Hate Women,\\" was perhaps more appropriate, though obviously unsuited for the not-very-adventurous U.S. market.) More conservative readers will probably be annoyed by the book's politics, and I suppose even liberal readers might find the political content intrusive; I didn't mind it, because I tended to agree with Larsson's points and I like social commentary, even when it's in-your-face.<br /><br />That said, I did have some major problems with the book. The prose style, at least in translation, is rather plain and uninspiring. (Raymond Chandler, this ain't.) The beginning of the book is too slow, and the ending drags on too long after the major mystery has been solved. Also, I wasn't keen on the solution to the mystery; the revelation of the \\"bad guy\\" somehow didn't have much impact for me. Still, a lot of mysteries are disappointing in this regard ... the chase is almost always more satisfying than the capture.<br /><br />In the end, I'd recommend \\"Dragon Tattoo\\" to the other skeptics out there. The book isn't quite as good as you've heard -- Lisbeth Salander in particular is nowhere near as \\"original\\" a character as people claim she is -- but it is pretty cool. I'm impressed that the mystery genre still holds some pleasant surprises, after all these decades...jor problems with the book. B007DJUO8O|5.0|Storm Corrosion, the collaboration between Mikael Akerfeldt of Opeth and Steven Wilson of Porcupine Tree, offers some intriguing, unpredictable journeys into what I would describe as mood music.  A few words that occur to me while listening are dreamlike, lush, serene, hypnotic, mellow, haunting, ethereal, exotic, and, at times, dark.  Beautiful acoustic work and captivating ambient passages intertwine for some entrancing zone-out music; it's easy to get lost in the soft, poignant melodies of each song.  For me, it took a few listens to really absorb the multiple layers of sound and appreciate how it all flows together.  I listened to the first track Drag Ropes once and thought it was ok, then I listened to it a second time and thought it was pretty great.  By the third time, I loved it, and the rest of the album had much the same effect.  Now I have to hear it every day, sometimes more than once.<br /><br />The CD's six songs alternate between strange, surreal ambience and delicate, melancholic folk.  There's a stark contrast from dark to light between the first two songs, Drag Ropes and the album's title track.  Drag Ropes is at times nightmarish, especially coupled with the Tim Burton-esque video released for it, while Storm Corrosion is a beautiful track, its haunting acoustic reminiscent of Opeth's Orchid (or even Led Zeppelin or early Black Sabbath).  The tone shifts back into darkness with Hag's rhythmic pulse and odd section of semi-heavy guitars and the album's only spurt of drumming.  Then, Happy mixes the strangely warped acoustic sound of Watershed with lighter, Still Life melodies akin to songs like The Moor or Benighted.  After this, it's back into more trippy territory with Lock Howl, a brilliant instrumental that I wish was at least twice as long as it is.  Ljudet Innan closes soft and serene, with some of Mikael's most ambitious vocals to date in the opening minutes.  My only complaint is that this album isn't longer, the six songs clocking in at close to 48 minutes.  I would've gladly taken a couple more ten-minute tracks.  If you get the special edition, it has instrumental versions of all the songs, but I just can't seem to get enough of this music.  Great stuff by Mikael and Steven.ose to 48 minutes. B0014ESMCY|5.0|For musicians, the fact that Scott LaFaro is on this album is reason to study it. LaFaro was one of the promising bassists who did not leave behind a large body of recordings before his untimely end.<br /><br />However, even had Lafaro lived to a ripe old age this is still an important album for a number of reasons. There is a discernible change in Bill Evans' playing in the ensuing months between Kind of Blue and this album. On Kind of Blue his solos were tentative at best, and when hard swinging was required on \\"Freddie Freeloader\\" Miles had Wynton Kelly sit in on that track. That is not to say that Evans' contribution on Kind of Blue was substandard because it wasn't. The flowing, mountain stream quality that Miles specifically wanted made that album work. That same quality in Bill Evans' playing is present on this album, but he swings and seems to exhibit boldness and confidence that was lacking just eight months prior.<br /><br />The real beauty is the trio as a unit. Motian-LaFaro-Evans is locked in as a unit and the music on every track is not only breathtaking, but groundbreaking because everyone is playing as equals with a single mind instead of in a formulaic piano trio format.<br /><br />There are a number of reasons to own and delve deep into this album: the music, the musicians, the musical direction, and other reasons that I have not thought of, but are as valid.

1590171993|5.0|I agree wholeheartedly with the reviewers who loved John Williams' Stoner.  it is heartachingly beautiful and sumptuously written.  The simple, \\"domestic\\" bare-bones of William Stoner's existence are superficially unremarkable.  However in their simplicity lies their universality:  I think the many who loved this book and the few who hated it, see in the protagonist's life their own stories.<br /><br />At times I found William Stoner's laconic passivity maddening.  The occasional spark to pursue happiness--a willingness to leave life down on the farm to pursue a life in academia is at first encouraging.  So too is the earnestness with which he pursues Edith, his first love and later wife.  Both examples though prove to be uncharacteristic.  William Stoner is a man to whom life happens--not one who goes out and pursues goals with any avidity or end result in mind.  When his marriage to Edith has failed yet she returns to reassert control over their daughter Grace, Stoner fails to fight for the daughter he has raised or for their love.  He lets himself be shunted aside, and the little girl to lose the love of a devoted parent.<br /><br />When he finds something like love with fellow teacher Katherine Driscoll he lets Lomax his nemesis drive her away.  He says he continues on in his loveless marriage and disappointing career for some foolish sense of being true to what he is.  Lonely?  A failure?  Soon however Williams' beautiful prose and generous nature began to work their magic.  If Stoner is a lonely failure he is a well-intended one. Not mean-spirited like his nemesis Lomax, a man physically,  emotionally and spiritually disfigured.  Nor is he flighty or abusive and hurtful like his angry and at times unstable wife.  Stoner always does his best, and tries to do right.  By his students, and by his daughter.  His failures are frequent, but so too are all of ours.  If at times he lacks courage, so do we all.  In the end he can look back on his life and if asked \\"who did you harm?\\"  He can truly say, \\"very few.\\" If asked \\"did you do your best?\\" He can truly answer, \\"nearly always.\\"  And if his best wasn't very brilliant, well, neither is ours.<br /><br />John Williams has created a marvelous portrait of every good man--a failure who has at least tried, again and again, to do right.did you harm?\\"  He can truly say, \\"very few.\\" If asked \\"did you do your best?\\" He can truly answer, \\"nearly always.\\"  And if his best wasn't very brilliant, well, neither is ours.<br /><br />John Williams has created a marvelous portrait of every good man--a failure who has at least tried, again and again, to do right.

B0077PBPJ6|3.0|England is at war, and Stephen Wraysford (Eddie Redmayne) is on the front lines. In command of a group of enterprising young men digging tunnels in order to get nearer foreign forces, Stephen spends most of his time above ground... until a superior officer (Matthew Goode) tells him to take to the tunnels. His timing could not be worse. Gunfire penetrates the underground shaft, leaving him badly wounded...<br /><br />But conscious to remember a much happier time... when he was employed at a textile factory in France. His employer is the wealthy, older Ren&eacute; Azaire but it is his lovely young wife Isabelle (Cl&eacute;mence Po&eacute;sy) who most attracts Stephen's attention. Quiet and self-contained, she can often be heard weeping at night. Her stepdaughter is not much younger than she is, and is romantically interested in Stephen... but he has eyes only for the beautiful young woman who suffers so much in silence. As their affair unfolds and carries them both into desperate times, the audience travels back and forth between earlier simplicity and later turmoil during wartime. Many films employ this tactic but few accomplish it with as gentle of grace as Birdsong.<br /><br />Though I have not read the book, I understand that certain things, including the style of the narrative, have been changed. Fans debate heatedly over whether or not the cast is appropriate... I found them a lovely couple to watch with natural chemistry that sparks through lingering glances more than passionate love scenes. One could argue that some of these glances are too long, that eyes meeting over dinner or on the river with music dramatically swelling in the background can at times be a bit dull... and yes, it is true that it prolongs things considerably. The photography is glorious and the costuming exquisite. Too bad it misses the point of the novel, by making it seem as if they are in love. The book is about lust. The miniseries transforms it into forbidden love. There is a distinction between the two.<br /><br />From a purely entertainment perspective, the first episode is terrific, and the second is considerably less so, in part because the wartime scenes are tedious and the abrupt nature of how the relationship ends is emotionally unsatisfying (we learn later the reason for her flight, but there is little build-up). Much of the last hour is downright depressing as we watch his friends die in battle, some of them brutally. The first half of this story was solid but in the second half it stumbled and lost its way, most of the tension going right along with it. Stephen cannot carry the story on his own without Isabelle, and she is absent for the most part in the last hour and a half. It needed a stronger narrative style to go with its beautiful costumes and exquisite period setting. But perhaps the most troubling is that neither character learns anything from their misbehavior or feels remorse for their actions. For me, that left the story feeling empty since when stripped of any redeeming value or moral messages, any adulterous love affair just becomes a story of extreme selfishness.<br /><br />Parental Concerns: I presume this is the original, uncut British release that contains graphic sexual content (including oral), and nudity. And it delivers.<br /><br />Chapters like \\"Give Them the Fish\\" and \\"An Offer You Can't Refuse\\" are written for first-time business owners. Yet, they also offer a gentle reminder to those of us who filed away our business planning exercises a year ago.<br /><br />The book is aspirational, while being specific and detailed on the steps any aspiring micro business owner should talk to set themselves up for success.<br /><br />It's important to note that the $100 Startup is based on more than Chris Guillebeaus's personal experience. I'm a fan of his, but I tend to get turned off when writers and bloggers assume that works for you is right for me. Chris successfully dodged that bullet by including 50 case studies in the book, which were culled from a group of 1,500. The strategies and tactics for building your business aren't good \\"because Chris says so,\\" they're proven by hundreds of success stories.<br /><br />Additionally, Chris is an excellent storyteller and writer. She has left her husband Peter Florrick (Chris Noth) after finding out he had a one-night stand years ago with Kalinda Sharma (Archie Panjaba), the firm's mysterious private investigator. In spite of the fact that Kalinda did not know Alicia then, this explosive revelation makes it impossible for Alicia to be anywhere near Kalinda without feeling an overwhelming urge to scream at her friend or burst into tears--which is a real problem since they have to work together on cases at Lockhart/Gardner.<br /><br />Kalinda, who normally is a really tough nut, is shown at the end of Season 2 visibly heartbroken over losing her friendship with Alicia, which was clearly one of the closest relationships she'd ever had. On the heels of what Alicia sees as a double betrayal, she has kicked Peter out of their apartment and dumped him and his possessions into an apartment she obtained for him without his prior knowledge. As apparent retaliation, Peter, who has just been re-elected to his old office as State's Attorney, strictly because of Alicia's high-profile loyalty and support during his campaign, has hired Cary Agos (Matt Czuchry) to be his second-in-command. Cary has been Alicia's enemy since Season 1 when she beat him out for an associate position at Lockhart/Gardner.<br /><br />Alicia has begun talks with Lockhart/Gardner's barracuda divorce attorney, David Lee (the immensely talented Zach Grenier), and launched a torrid affair with Will Gardner (Josh Charles). For multiple reasons, Alicia and Will opt to keep their affair top secret: he is her boss, she is still legally married to Peter, and she doesn't want her kids to know about the affair.<br /><br />This show does a fantastic job this entire season, as it did the previous two seasons, delivering interesting cases for Lockhart/Gardner to represent. The series's excellent writers (led by award-winning, writer/producer, husband-and-wife team, Michelle and Robert King) keep these cases enthralling to viewers by offering spicy--and sometimes humorous--conflicts for the lawyers, such as client chicanery to be worked around, in-house rivalries threatening to torpedo the success of a given case, or mysteries for the ruthlessly capable Kalinda to solve in order to prove a client's innocence or win a case. In addition, whatever the overarching legal plot, it always deftly intertwines with several personal-relationship subplots every week. All of the main characters are linked to each other and numerous other subcharacters in an ever expanding web of secrets, lies and conniving. This could easily become confusing rather than pleasantly intriguing in less skillful hands than those of the show's writing team, but they never drop any plot threads, and never allow the show to slide into outright soapiness, a failing that seems to afflict the vast majority of nighttime drama.<br /><br />The acting continues to be terrific. I concur 100% with the Emmy wins of Julianna Margulies for Outstanding Lead Actress in a Drama Series, Archie Panjabi for Outstanding Supporting Actress in a Drama Series, and the nominations of Josh Charles as Outstanding Supporting Actor in a Drama Series, and Christine Baranski, as Diane Lockhart, for Outstanding Supporting Actress in a Drama Series.<br /><br />Though Matt Czuchry has not yet been nominated for an Emmy for this show, he contributes greatly to its success by his capable handling of his recurring role as ambitious attorney, Cary Agos. I quite enjoyed the growth arc of his relationship with Alicia this season. Czuchry's outstanding ability to portray Cary's intense emotions through subtle facial expressions--in keeping with the poker face that men in general employ, and lawyers especially are forced to cultivate--never fails to entertain.<br /><br />Mary Beth Peil as Jackie Florrick continues her excellent work as the ambitious mother of Alicia's husband, Peter, and Peter also gets a lot of stage time this season as he contemplates a run for the governor's office. This goal is made all the more fascinating because it is impossible to achieve without the cooperation of both Alicia and cagey political operative, Eli Gold.<br /><br />Eli is one of my favorites on a show overflowing with talented actors playing plum parts. Alan Cumming's portrayal of Eli is filled with delightful contradiction. Just when Eli's ruthlessness is about to push him over the edge into the territory of unsympathetic, irredeemable jerk, out peeks a soft-heartedness that is often quite funny to behold. After Season 1, Cumming received an Emmy nomination as Outstanding Guest Actor in a Drama Series. Apparently because his work was so exceptional that year, he was promoted to a recurring role, and for Season 2, he received an Emmy nomination for Outstanding Supporting Actor.<br /><br />I could go on and on about the caliber of the many actors with recurring guest roles, but two in particular deserve special attention: Michael J. Fox received his 12th Emmy nomination for his portrayal of attorney Louis Canning, who cynically attempts to gin up sympathy from judges and juries for his Parkinson-type symptoms in order to win his cases. 1932100660|2.0|Here's a pretty good article discussing the book:  [...]  I've attached the author's summary/conclusion:    In summary and conclusion...    Apart from his cherry-picked references for other studies (some of which don't back up the claims he cites them for), Campbell's strongest arguments against animal foods hinge heavily on:      Associations between cholesterol and disease, and    His discoveries regarding casein and cancer.    For #1, it seems Campbell never took the critical step of accounting for other disease-causing variables that tend to cluster with higher-cholesterol counties in the China Study--variables like schistosomiasis infection, industrial work hazards, increased hepatitis B infection, and other non-nutritional factors spurring chronic conditions. Areas with lower cholesterol, by contrast, tended to have fewer non-dietary risk factors, giving them an automatic advantage for preventing most cancers and heart disease. (The health threats in the lower-cholesterol areas were more related to poor living conditions, leading to greater rates of tuberculosis, pneumonia, intestinal obstruction, and so forth.)    Even if the correlations with cholesterol did remain after adjusting for these risk factors, it takes a profound leap in logic to link animal products with disease by way of blood cholesterol when the animal products themselves don't correlate with those diseases. If all three of these variables rose in unison, then hypotheses about animal foods raising disease risk via cholesterol could be justified. Yet the China Study data speaks for itself: Animal protein doesn't correspond with more disease, even in the highest animal food-eating counties--such as Tuoli, whose citizens chow down on 134 grams of animal protein per day.    Nor is the link between animal food consumption and cholesterol levels always as strong as Campbell implies. For instance, despite eating such massive amounts of animal foods, Tuoli county had the same average cholesterol levelas the near-vegan Shanyang county, and a had a slightly lower cholesterol than another near-vegan county called Taixing. (Both Shanyang and Taixing consumed less than 1 gram of animal protein per day, on average.) Clearly, the relationship between animal food consumption and blood cholesterol isn't always linear, and other factors play a role in raising or lowering levels.    For #2, Campbell's discoveries with casein and cancer, his work is no doubt revelatory. I give him props for dedicating so much of his life to a field of disease research that wasn't always well-received by the scientific community, and for pursuing so ardently the link between nutrition and health. Unfortunately, Campbell projects the results of his casein-cancer research onto all animal protein--a leap he does not justify with evidence or even sound logic.    As ample literature indicates, other forms of animal protein--particularly whey, another component of milk--may have strong anti-cancer properties. Some studies have examined the effect of whey and casein, side-by-side, on tumor growth and cancer, showing in nearly all cases that these two proteins have dramatically different effects on tumorigenesis (with whey being protective). A study Campbell helped conduct with one of his grad students in the 1980s showed that the cancer-promoting abilities of fish protein depended on what type of fat is consumed alongside it. The relationship between animal protein and cancer is obviously complex, situationally dependent, and bound with other substances found in animal foods--making it impossible extrapolate anything universal from a link between isolated casein and cancer.    On page 106 of his book, Campbell makes a statement I wholeheartedly agree with:    Everything in food works together to create health or disease. The more we think that a single chemical characterizes a whole food, the more we stray into idiocy.    It seems ironic that Campbell censures reductionism in nutritional science,yet uses that very reductionism to condemn an entire class of foods (animal products) based on the behavior of one substance in isolation (casein).    In sum, \\"The China Study\\" is a compelling collection of carefully chosen data. Unfortunately for both health seekers and the scientific community, Campbell appears to exclude relevant information when it indicts plant foods as causative of disease, or when it shows potential benefits for animal products. This presents readers with a strongly misleading interpretation of the original China Study data, as well as a slanted perspective of nutritional research from other arenas (including some that Campbell himself conducted).    In rebuttals to previous criticism on \\"The China Study,\\" Campbell seems to use his curriculum vitae as reason his word should be trusted above that of his critics. His education and experience is no doubt impressive, but the \\"Trust me, I'm a scientist\\" argument is a profoundly weak one. It doesn't require a PhD to be a critical thinker, nor does a laundry list of credentials prevent a person from falling victim to biased thinking. Ultimately, I believe Campbell was influenced by his own expectations about animal protein and disease, leading him to seek out specific correlations in the China Study data (and elsewhere) to confirm his predictions.    It's no surprise \\"The China Study\\" has been so widely embraced within the vegan and vegetarian community: It says point-blank what any vegan wants to hear--that there's scientific rationale for avoiding all animal foods. That even small amounts of animal protein are harmful. That an ethical ideal can be completely wed with health. These are exciting things to hear for anyone trying to justify a plant-only diet, and it's for this reason I believe \\"The China Study\\" has not received as much critical analysis as it deserves, especially from some of the great thinkers in the vegetarian world. Hopefully this critique has shed some light on the book's problemsand will lead others to examine the data for themselves.

We used a lot of what I read about in his other book, The Art of Non-Conformity, in setting up our business model. You don't need to spend millions, have tons of employees, and have an 80-page business plan to start a business. If you're long-time readers of the blog, you know that it's all about hustle-hustle-hustle, and the book has a whole chapter (Chapter 9) on hustling, or as Chris defines it: \\"building interest and attracting attention.\\"<br /><br />Filled with case studies of others entrepreneurs who all make at least $50k a year from their small businesses, this book gives you the blueprint to start your own little company based on something you love.  The first part of the book examines the lean and mean $100 startup business model, and also leads you through how and why you might want to start up your own (side) business.<br /><br />The second part of the book talks about the basics behind launching a product, marketing, getting your stuff out there, unconventional fundraising, and one-page business plans.  Lots of good stuff here to get you up and running.<br /><br />The third section of the book is about expanding (or choosing not to), leveraging your existing resources, and talks about what to think about for the long haul.  Throughout all the sections, Chris intersperses advice with the case studies I mentioned earlier.<br /><br />While I wish there was a bit more specific advice and less storytelling, I do realize that specific advice for me doesn't apply to everyone, and vice versa.  Chris did the best job of straddling that fine line between sharing what worked for others and what might work for you.  Also, as someone who has read a lot of online marketing material and bought courses, this book didn't offer many mid-tier/higher level advice, but then again, that's not who this is for.<br /><br />This book is for people who haven't taken that first step towards becoming entrepreneurs, and want to learn more about the process before they dive right in.  I think this would also be great for any college-age students who are getting a bit disenchanted with the job market out there right now.

0307951529|5.0|Inspiring, practical, entertaining--The $100 Startup stands head and shoulders above most other business books out there. You know the ones I'm talking. They sound a clarion call for an unconventional life but then leave you stranded without any real, nuts-and-bolts strategies for making that life happen.<br /><br />This book is different. Chris Guillebeau has stitched together a fascinating collection of success stories from fascinating people whose professional paths are as different from each other as Chris's is from a 9-to-5 desk jockey. He writes honestly about the elbow grease involved with starting a business without making the book some grim tale of entrepreneurial woes, but at the same time he doesn't diminish the accomplishments of the many people he interviewed by portraying them as the \\"lucky\\" ones. The message is pretty straightforward: What do you care about? What are you good at? What needs can you meet in a market associated with those two? Answer these questions, and then get started.<br /><br />The $100 Startup provides a balanced understanding of the mental, professional, and even emotional changes--discontentment, anyone?--that need to happen so that we can find more satisfaction in our work and enjoy more freedom in our lives.<br /><br />I've already started several successful businesses, yet I still found myself eagerly turning pages, taking notes, and planning my next adventure. If you want both a wakeup call and a step-by-step plan, then The $100 Startup is for you.

0307352145|5.0|Cain has written a remarkable book that is both thoroughly researched and beneficial not only to introverts but to extroverts as well.  While \\"Quiet\\" is not a biography, Cain uses many biographical references juxtaposed with psychological studies to support her argument that introverts are overlooked, oppressed even,  in Western society despite having just as much, even more in some cases, to offer than extroverts.  Of course Cain is writing subjectively being an introvert herself.  However, she does not use the book as a platform to bash extroverts.  She contends that extroverts and introverts both have much to offer and rather than societies favoring one personality type over another there needs to be partnership between both.  She argues that in current Western mentality, extroverts are exalted while introverts are viewed as weak or lacking in character.  According to Cain, this painfully overlooks the fact that there is strength in the quiet introvert.<br /><br />In her introduction Cain writes about the partnership of Rosa Parks and Dr. Martin Luther King Jr., \\"Parks herself seemed aware of this paradox, calling her autobiography \\"Quiet Strength\\"--a title that challenges us to question our assumptions.  Why shouldn't quiet be strong?  And what else can quiet do that we don't give it credit for?...Take the partnership of Rosa Parks and Martin Luther King Jr.: a formidable orator refusing to give up his seat on a segregated bus wouldn't have had the same effect as a modest woman who'd clearly prefer to keep silent but for the exigencies of the situation...Yet today we make room for a remarkably narrow range of personality styles.  We're told that to be great is to be bold, to be happy is to be sociable.  We see ourselves as a nation of extroverts--which means that we've lost sight of who we really are\\"(Pg. 2-3).<br /><br />The book is divided into four sections: 1) The Extrovert Ideal; 2) Your Biology, Your Self?; 3) Do All Cultures Have An Extrovert Ideal; 4) How To Love, How To Work. In the first section Cain demonstrates how extroversion has come to be the standard for Western society.  Cain convincingly argues that the rise of the Extrovert Ideal began with Dale Carnegie.  She writes, \\"Carnegie's metamorphosis from farmboy to salesman to public-speaking icon is also the story of the rise of the extrovert ideal...America had shifted from what the influential cultural historian Warren Susman called a Culture of Character to a Culture of Personality...when they embraced the Culture of Personality, Americans started to focus on how others perceived them.  They became captivated by people who were bold and entertaining\\" (Pg. 20-21).  Cain also shares stories about attending a Tony Robbins Unleash-The-Power-Within seminar and visiting Rick Warren's Saddleback Church to further support her contention of the Extrovert Ideal.<br /><br />Cain's distaste for the Tony Robbins seminar and her experience with  Saddleback Church resonate with me--I completely indentify with her!  I feel like these kinds of institutions are pushed upon us by society and Cain convincingly argues that while these institutions may be good for extroverts they are not the best environments for introverts to thrive and our western society would be much better served if this was recognized resulting in more introverted-friendly institutions or environments.<br /><br />Having established that the West lives within the paradigm of the Extrovert Ideal, Cain looks at the psychology and science related to extroversion and introversion in the second section.  The studies that Cain presents are both enlightening and fascinating.  It was interesting for me at least to learn that evidence suggests that 40 to 50 percent of introvertedness and extrovertedness is genetic (Pg. 108).  I was relieved to learn that a third to half of Westerners are introverted.  Moreover, the fact that introvertedness can be tied to genetics gave more credence to the fact that I am normal.Finally, as a quick aside to this section if one is not familiar with the life of Eleanor Roosevelt the brief, yet fascinating, biography found at the beginning of chapter 6 alone makes Cain's book worth purchasing.<br /><br />In the third section, Cain looks at Eastern cultures and shows that the Extrovert Ideal is not universal.  In this section she spends time interviewing Asian students from Harvard's business school and high school students from a public school system in California.  Cain writes, \\"Individuals in Asia see themselves as part of a greater whole--whether family, corporation, or community--and place tremendous value on harmony within their group.  They often subordinate their own desires to the group's interest accepting their place in its hierarchy.  Western culture, by contrast, is organized around the individual\\" (Pg. 188-189).  If there is one area that lacked research in this book this would be it--her research seemed to be limited to those from Eastern cultural backgrounds residing in our Western culture.  It would have been interesting to read about a more thorough discussion of firsthand experience with Eastern cultures.<br /><br />The fourth section can be best summed up in Cain's words, \\"Probably the most common--and damaging--misunderstanding about personality type is that introverts are antisocial and extroverts are pro-social.  But as we've seen, neither formulation is correct; introverts and extroverts are differently social\\" (Pg. 226).  Cain also argues that there is a time for introverts to put on an extroverted face, she writes, \\"Yes, we are only pretending to be extroverts, and yes, such inauthenticity can be morally ambiguous (not to mention exhausting), but if it's in the service of love or a professional calling, then we're just as Shakespeare advised...'To thine own self be true'\\" (Pg. 210).<br /><br />At one point in the book I wrote in the margins, \\"When you boil it down this book is essentially about relationships.\\"  Of course no one is a pure introvert nor a pure extrovert but all of us are going to lean one direction or the other and fall on different points along the spectrum.  Cain's book has helped me realize that if we are more sensitive to these leanings we can be more productive as a society and more importantly we can have more meaning in our lives because our relationships will be deeper.<br /><br />Frankly, this book spoke to me.  For my whole life I have struggled with the fact that I am a \\"quiet\\" person and very introverted--not necessarily shy but, yes, introverted.  I was frustrated that I did not fit what seems to be the norm for our society.  After reading Cain's book I am both thankful for who I am and much more comfortable with who I am.  For the first time I believe that I have a lot to offer not in spite of my introvertedness but because of my introvertedness.  Moreover, this book helped lessen my animosity toward extroverts because I no longer need to be envious of them and this will allow me to approach them in love and community.  I would recommend this book to anyone and everyone!<br /><br />I received this book free from WaterBrook Multnomah for the purposes of this review.

B0059XTV4Y|4.0|The much maligned, brief theatrical film by Madonna - W.E. - fairs better on the small screen than it likely did in the movie houses. The stories are bifurcated, each one resembling a television creation - one a docudrama biopic, the other a contemporary soap opera.  That Madonna, who directed and wrote the screenplay with Alek Keshishian, decided to mix the two stories is a bit daring but in some ways it works very well.  In other ways the parallel stories seem like time traveling cars on the same highway that never quite travel at the same speed or quality.<br /><br />The film mixes the notorious affair between King Edward VIII and American divorc&eacute;e Wallis Simpson with a contemporary romance between a married woman and a Russian security guard. The time is 1998 and at an auction of the estate of the Duke and Duchess of Windsor unhappily married ex-Sotheby employee Wally Winthrop (Abbie Cornish) becomes obsessed by their historic love story. Her own marriage to womanizing, abusive psychiatrist William (Richard Coyle) undermines her feelings of worth and as she learns more about the sacrifices involved in the famous affair, she gains her own courage to find happiness.<br /><br />The film flips back and forth between the present and the 1930s and it is the historical aspect of the film that is almost flawless. We get to know Wallis Simpson (in a brilliant portrayal by Andrea Riseborough) and understand her failed first two marriages (at the time we meet her she is still married to Ernest Simpson played by David Harbour), and see the American sizzle that made her the talk of England. When Wallis wrangles her way to meet Prince Edward, better known as David, (James D'Arcy) there is a chemistry that develops to the point of passion and ultimately leads to Wallis divorcing Ernest to marry Edward - a deed that leads to Edward's abdication of the throne for `the woman I love', which he had assumed when King George V (James Fox) dies, to his stammering brother Bertie (Laurence Fox) and his caustic wife Elizabeth (Natalie Dormer).  The paparazzi make their life miserable and the couple is not allowed to return to England until Edward dies, with the faithful Wallis supportively by his side through 36 years of marriage.<br /><br />Wally - meanwhile - longs to be pregnant but sustains such abuse from William that she ultimately yields to the loving friendship the auction house Russian security guard Evgeni (Oscar Isaac) and begins her life again. 0307951529|4.0|Chris Guillebeau's philosophy is that the most rewarding work takes work, and that it should be done for personal fulfillment as much as for financial freedom. The 100 or so businesses used as case studies in the book reinforce this philosophy--each of these microbusinesses employs five or fewer employees (many are solopreneurs), and most are designed to stay that way.<br /><br />This is not, in other words, a book about building a massive, franchised empire from a single taco stand, nor designing killer iOS apps that get bought by Facebook for a billion dollars: it's about helping you to come up with a solid idea at the intersection of your passion and a customer's need to create a business that can support your full range of needs.<br /><br />To that end, the book provides a wealth of tested concepts and tools, intertwined with stories illustrating how they apply to creating success in a small business. There are checklists for evaluating the business-worthiness of your ideas and for prepping a product launch. There are formulas for constructing a marketing offer or creating a self-published work. There are charts that explain the different types of sales methods and that map the difference between passions that are fun for you and passions that will work in the marketplace.<br /><br />As the author notes, this is not a book meant to be read for information only; it's intended as an actual blueprint for building a business, which means you'll need to supply the elbow grease. And at just over 300 pages, it's unlikely to serve as a complete guide to running a business, even the very particular kind of service-driven, internet-fueled micro-minibusiness featured inside.<br /><br />But what The $100 Startup lacks in depth it makes up for in breadth and readability. There's more than enough inside to get you started, and even well on your way, if you're willing to do the work.

1847669816|5.0|[[VIDEOID:mo2H6CTKE589IZ1]]<br /><br />FOR ALL WHO SUPPORT PROTESTS AND PROTESTERS -WITH ACCOMPANYING ONLINE UPDATING SERVICE<br /><br />An appreciation by Phillip Taylor MBE and Elizabeth Taylor of Richmond Green Chambers<br /><br />The authors of this book; three barristers and a solicitor are all committed to protecting and upholding the rights of protestors.  The result of their efforts is this clear and accessible guide to protest law, the only handbook available, in fact, which covers protest law in both its civil and criminal aspects.<br /><br />Recently published by Bloomsbury Professional, the book examines and focuses upon the rights of protesters under the European Convention on Human Rights, which -- as the admirable Shami Chackrabarti, together with James Welch of `Liberty' point out in the Foreword -- established a positive right to protest for the first time in the United Kingdom, specifically under Articles 10 (freedom of expression) and 11 (freedom of assembly).<br /><br />It's a sobering thought that previous protests throughout history, at least in a Western context, have not had a Human Rights Act as such, to protect their activities, or their rights.  The slave army of Spartacus in ancient Rome was defeated, with each of the several thousand survivors eventually crucified.  The Peasants' Revolt of 1381 was quashed and its leaders, including Wat Tyler subsequently executed.<br /><br />And as an example of the voice of the lone protestor in the wilderness, we recall the story of one William Prynne, a barrister at Lincoln's Inn, who roughly in the 1640s, wrote a series of pamphlets protesting against the excesses of Charles I.  For his efforts, he endured the dismemberment of his ears, followed by the branding of both cheeks with `SL' for `Seditious Libeller'.  Didn't stop him though; he carried on pamphleteering.<br /><br />Closer to our own time, there have been any number of protests, from the Chartists, to the suffragist movement, to the more than 20 year Civil Rights movement in the US and the anti-Vietnam protests and so forth, including the dissenters against the now defunct Soviet Union and latterly, the thousands of participants in the `Arab Spring.'<br /><br />Just contemplating the raw courage and personal sacrifice of this assortment of protesters past and present is a humbling experience, which frankly, reminds some of us that you don't really have to be all that brave anymore to protest in a free society - and good thing too.<br /><br />Your rights, as outlined in this scholarly and eminently readable and accessible handbook, are protected, at least in law, although in reality, the actual risks faced by protesters still remain very real, with protesters often ending up arrested and/or in court.<br /><br />At the moment, what might be termed the law of protest remains a niche area in which many practitioners have little experience.  This book therefore, will almost certainly emerge as a landmark text which offers a guide to the legal issues involved, including police powers, human rights, criminal law, the legality of administrative action and elements of land law and more.<br /><br />Within its approximately 500 pages, the book offers a wide range of research resources, including tables of statutes, statutory instruments and cases, plus five useful appendices, not to mention the accompanying updating service available online when you purchase the book, which certainly you should do if you find yourself involved professionally in this often vexed area of law. 0439023483|4.0|As many have said, I loved the premise of this book. Borrowing from Lord of the Flies, the book chronicles a young woman's experience as she is swept up into a cruel, gladiator-like battle royal between fellow teens from different districts. All of this occurs at the fancy of a ruthless, totalitarian regime that wants to punish the districts for staging a rebellion against their leadership many decades prior. This book is extremely engaging, but it is not without faults. Told in the first person, we end up gaining the protagonist's perspective fully but lose much of what goes on behind the scenes. This serves to propel the action forward---but ultimately fails to put those experiences into a higher context. Fact is, there is/was a lot going on behing the scenes. For example, I would have loved to get more of a sense of what was going through the Chief Gamemaker's mind. Also would have loved to know a little bit more about the Capitol.<br /><br />Collins has a way with action sequences (some of which had me on the edge of my seat)--but makes little attempt to develop any of her characters beyond the superficial. I never found myself believing in the love triangle--in part because I never got to know Gale. A few more introspective moments involving Peeta might have also given me reason to care more about him as a charater. Rue also came and went without any significant character development. Having said this, I found this to be one of the more memorable books that I have read in some time. I riffled through it on a plane trip and felt the need to finish it as soon as I got a chance (which meant reading long into the night). That is the mark of a good book....but not a great one.---Just rue (no pun intended) the missed opportunities to slow the book down a little, and cater a little more to an older readership who seeks a little more than what was given.

In these two areas primarily, cohabitants certainly don't have the same rights as married couples, not yet anyway, due to current public policy.<br /><br />Not many couples realize this of course.  Predictably, and understandably, when they do decide to cohabit, they are not apt to rush headlong into a lawyer's office at the heady start of their relationship just to find out what their rights are in law.  The usual pattern for cohabitants unfortunately, is to consult lawyers or advisers only when their relationship starts to come unstuck, or when there's a sudden crisis.<br /><br />At this stage, overwhelming complexities can emerge relating to any number of issues of dispute, from property rights (i.e. who owns what?) ownership of chattels, taxation and parental responsibilities, to pension rights, welfare benefits, separation agreements, death and succession and much more besides.<br /><br />All this notwithstanding, the book sensibly takes a `prevention is better than cure' approach, especially in the chapter on ante-nuptial agreements. The section on welfare benefits is also new and all the precedents covered are contained in the accompanying CD ROM.<br /><br />Scholarly, thorough and readable, this is a book designed with the needs of the busy practitioner in mind. Almost all of the big obstacles to the success of the heroes get resolved at the very end and only minor conflicts are left. In essence, the author has to start all over again which seems like an extra chore when writing a long fantasy series.<br /><br />The tale focuses upon a group of sophisticated rogues who are going to pull off the ultimate heist in a dystopian fantasy world namely slay the evil ruler, wipe out his many minions and resources and then make off with all of the treasury funds. Oh, yeah, they're going to rule the capitol city, too. The problem here is that the peasants who they want to revolt against the empire are underfed and magically reduced in any emotions that might make them feel like rebelling. Additionally, most of the people with magical powers are nobles and they side with the evil ruler. Semi powerful types are known as Misted and full fledged magical types are Mistborn. The only way to get this power is to have noble blood in you so as a result there are a tiny minority of peasants who have these powers but don't know it or come into it over time. Of course, any such children found out by the evil empire are put to death in order to control the playing field.<br /><br />Speaking of the magic that was probably the most intriguing part of the book for those who have read a lot of fantasy and get the same magic systems over and over. Magic is fueled by trace minerals that are ingested by those with the power. A Misted can only ingest one type of metal but a Mistborn can take as many trace minerals as desired (there are a standard 8 with some surprises). Mistborn are rare even in the nobility and as a result are highly prized by the powerful.<br /><br />Anyway, said thieving crew go about dismantling the capitol city and while certain parts of it made enough sense other parts did not.I will detail this more in the spoilers section at the very bottom. 0393088774|3.0|In a nutshell, Krugman says that, contrary to popular dogma, the national debt is not our greatest problem, rather, the greatest problem we face right now is unemployment and austerity is not the solution, instead, another much larger stimulus, along with a proper dosage of inflation, is what can cure what ails us.<br /><br />He has some good points to make. Austerity did not work during the Great Depression and it's not working in either England or anywhere else in Europe right now where it's being applied. On the other hand, I remain skeptical about the idea of another (larger) stimulus. He argues that it \\"worked\\" for the Great Depression with the giant expansion in government spending during WWII, which did get people working and back on their feet...during WWII. The evidence that this is what sustained growth and employment thereafter, however, is not as cut and dried as he makes it all seem and it's not at all clear that another much larger stimulus would do anything more than create larger temporary effects in the present day either. Krugman criticizes the \\"Austerians\\" for believing in the \\"Confidence Fairy\\", but seems to have unwittingly invoked the same delusional thinking when proposing his own solutions. Inflation, for example, which he proposes as another useful tool, (assuming that you can keep it inside the gateway from Pandora's Box by holding it steady at 4%), might have a more long term impact on trimming down the debt, but not if you are piling even more debt onto it at the same time.<br /><br />Krugman correctly identifies umemployment and lack of demand as being by far the greater problem for the moment, when juxtaposed next to the debt, which is more of a long term problem (but a significant one all the same). However, there are no clear and easy solutions to any of these problems, although Krugman would like us to think so, if only we were willing to drink the kool-aid.

0061537969|5.0|Garth Stein starts off with a cliche. A set in stone 'no-no' of the writing school. NEVER WRITE FROM THE POINT OF VIEW OF AN ANIMAL. I have a secret. I love cliche's. What I love more than cliche's is watching people use them and use them well. Garth Stein has shattered the rule. This book is simply breathtaking. It is written in the point of view of a dog, but not just any dog. This is a dog of deep philosophical leanings, of good intentions and loyal and trusted and true as all good dogs are and should be. But the story isn't really about Enzo. It's about his owner (Do you see? Do you see? I'm welling up as I write this review!) a race car driver, who marries, has a child, and then watches as the life slowly ebbs from his beautiful and beloved wife's body. While his life shatters, first by tragedy, and then by the selfish machinations of those around him, Enzo remains at his side, sustaining him through the hard times, helping him to persevere, no matter the obstacles. And yet Enzo has some personal motivations of his own. He wishes to be reincarnated. The years in his life are limited. He wishes, as is right, I suppose, for any good and faithful dog to do, to remain with his master always. Only next time, if he is very, very, very good...he will come back as a man.<br /><br />This story, for all its seeming simplicity, is a many layered thing. You have the dog, you have the family, all typical stuff. But then you have the racing, and the end of life issues, and woven together it makes for a brilliant analogy of life and love and loss and triumph. This is not my typical read. Had I picked it up in a store, I would have laid it back down again. But I'm so glad I didn't. It was loaned to me by a neighbour, and I'll always be grateful to her for sharing with me this book, which she took into her heart, and now I have taken into mine.<br /><br />Enzo lives on. Never to be forgotten.

0553386794|5.0|I was going to rate this book 4/5 but then I realized that if a book is worth a five it should be given a five regardless if it has flaws. I'm not out to prove which books are 'technically' but which books are the best. Therefore this book is a great book and a must read for any adult despite small flaws. Five/Five stars, Ten/Five stars, Twenty/Five stars.<br /><br />The Good:<br />As in my review title I think this book is hands down one of the most intelligent fantasy books I've yet read apart from the Lord of the Rings series. The corruption, the plot twists, the betrayal, the treason, the death, the war, the love, the struggle, the brotherhood, the symbolism, the character detail, the world detail, and the gigantic scope set up for this series is all top notch. It's almost Shakespearean tragedy, in a way, done in a contemporary style. I don't know if I've known in any other modern fantasy so many real and deep characters. I feel like I can empathize most and visualize them completely (even ones without a written point of view). Martin also does an incredible job of keeping the reader on track with the separate story-lines as each comes back to itself after an absence.<br />So on and so forth, all the wonderful comments made by every other five-star reviewer.<br /><br />The Bad:<br />Unfortunately there's one thing Martin seems to have a continual habit of doing in this book (and I hope not the rest in the series) which is forgetting his point of view - just for little sentences here and there. I'm almost certain there is at least one time in each chapter where he writes a sentence explaining something (a thought, a look, some detail, etc) that the current point of view could not know. Maybe I'm just picking nits or looking too closely, not looking closely enough, but over and over this writing flaw nagged - never very much, like I said one sentence in a chapter maybe two, but it was consistent through the entire novel.<br />Also small parts of the world I couldn't visualize completely: how do they measure years when seasons are multiple years long? or how does a world have such variable seasonal patterns? Are there miniature seasons that occur to follow a complete annual pattern underneath the greater climate change? Obviously I'm not asking Martin to provide a scientific answer to his world climate, but little things fit easily enough - change in harvests, other celestial bodies, etc that make the world make that much more sense. The former especially can be used merely in passing but provide a major world-explaining detail.<br />The last complaint I have (which other reviewers have mentioned) is that though the bulk of the story is very adult and a major theme seems to be loss of innocence, the sex in a number of cases seems very adolescent. I believe Martin makes an effort to be mature and succeeds at least once or twice (Catelyn's sex scenes and how he writes of Lord's bastards) sometimes it falls flat and tears me out of the story (most often concerning Daenerys). However it is fully possible I hold my standards higher than most.<br /><br />These three details I choose to point out are ultimately negligible in face of what actually makes this book so good. They are nitpicks and can be easily overlooked/forgotten within the same page they occur.<br />Read it, it's worth the money especially if you buy it used. I'm going to read the series in its entirety but I would be just as satisfied having read only this first novel.'t visualize completely: how do they measure years when seasons are multiple years long? or how does a world have such variable seasonal patterns?
